## Agent 6 — Continued Attack: Fixed-Prime Jet-Map Hitting Scale

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-prime phase model heuristically supports the countermodel, not the lower bound.}
}
\]

If the residual pair

\[
(R_1(t),R_2(t))
=
\left(
\Gamma_G(t),\Gamma_{G,2}(t)
\right)
\]

behaves like a transverse two-dimensional observable of the prime-phase flow, then the expected minimum over a height interval of length \(\asymp T\) is not polynomially small in \(Q=\log T\). It is exponentially small:

\[
\boxed{
\min_{t\in[T,2T]}\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\approx T^{-1/2}
=
e^{-Q/2}.
}
\]

That directly defeats the desired lower bound

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

So the fixed-coefficient Euler-product fork is leaning adversarial: unless actual \(\zeta\) has a repulsion mechanism forcing the prime-phase orbit away from the layered incidence set, the source-weighted lower bound is statistically implausible. This continues the arithmetic fork identified in the prior instruction: test whether fixed prime phases can hit the bad source-weighted jet set at polynomial or super-polynomial scale. 

---

# 1. Reformulate the target as a hitting problem

Let

\[
R_1(t):=\Gamma_G(t)
=
q''(t)-6q_0(t)+2q_0(t)^3,
\]

\[
R_2(t):=\Gamma_{G,2}(t)
=
(1-q_0(t)^2)q''(t)-\frac16q^{(4)}(t),
\]

and

\[
H(t):=y(t)q''(t).
\]

The desired source-weighted lower bound is

\[
\boxed{
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The bad set is

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
=
\{R_1=0,\ R_2=0,\ H\ne0\}.
\]

For the fixed-prime surrogate,

\[
L_X(t)
=
\sum_{p^k\le X}
(\log p)p^{-k/2}e^{-ikt\log p},
\]

the phase vector is

\[
\Theta(t)
=
(t\log p_1,\ldots,t\log p_N)\pmod{2\pi}.
\]

The relevant map is not the full torus point \(\Theta(t)\). It is the low-dimensional observable

\[
\Theta(t)\mapsto (R_1(t),R_2(t),H(t)).
\]

So the bad event is not full \(N\)-torus hitting. It is hitting a codimension-two residual set.

---

# 2. Generic codimension estimate

Assume the induced distribution of

\[
(R_1(t),R_2(t))
\]

has a smooth density near \((0,0)\), and that \(H(t)\) is nonzero with positive conditional probability near \(R_1=R_2=0\).

Then for small \(\varepsilon\),

\[
\mathbb P\left(\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon,\ |H|>c\right)
\asymp
\varepsilon^2.
\]

The number of effectively independent samples in an interval of length \(T\) is approximately

\[
M\asymp T\cdot \Omega,
\]

where \(\Omega\) is the effective bandwidth. For a prime sum with largest frequency \(\log X\),

\[
\Omega\asymp \log X.
\]

Even if we pessimistically take only

\[
M\asymp T,
\]

the expected minimum scale solves

\[
M\varepsilon^2\asymp1.
\]

Thus

\[
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp T^{-1/2}.
\]

Since

\[
Q=\log T,
\]

this is

\[
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp e^{-Q/2}.
\]

So the generic fixed-prime observable model predicts

\[
\boxed{
\min_{t\in[T,2T]}\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\ll Q^{-A}
}
\]

for every fixed \(A\), at large \(T\).

This is exactly the adversarial scale.

---

# 3. Why full torus hitting was the wrong obstruction

A previous concern was that to hit an \(e^{-Q}\)-ball in an \(N\)-torus one needs time

\[
e^{QN},
\]

which is impossible when \(N\) is large.

But the bad condition does **not** prescribe all prime phases. It imposes only two scalar residual conditions:

\[
R_1(t)\approx0,\qquad R_2(t)\approx0.
\]

If the map from prime phases to residuals is transverse, then many phase configurations hit the residual set. The relevant dimension is the codimension of the residual, not the number of active primes.

So the correct heuristic is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{bad residual hitting is low-codimension and should be common, unless arithmetic creates avoidance.}
}
\]

This is bad for the proof.

---

# 4. Source factor \(H=yq''\)

The full product requires

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|).
\]

The bad event can occur in two ways.

## Regime A: residual small, source live

If

\[
|H(t)|\asymp1
\]

and

\[
\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\asymp T^{-1/2},
\]

then

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\asymp T^{-1/2}.
\]

This defeats every polynomial lower bound in \(Q\).

## Regime B: source small

If \(H(t)\) itself has a one-dimensional zero-like distribution, then over \(M\asymp T\) samples its minimum scale is heuristically

\[
T^{-1}.
\]

Then even if \((R_1,R_2)\) are \(O(1)\),

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\asymp T^{-1}.
\]

Again this defeats every \(Q^{-A}\) bound.

Thus including \(H\) does not rescue the lower bound. It creates another way to fail.

---

# 5. What theorem would be needed?

The desired lower bound can only hold if actual \(\zeta\) has a strong **avoidance principle**:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the prime-phase orbit avoids the source-weighted layered incidence set at polynomial scale.}
}
\]

A theorem would need to say something like:

\[
\boxed{
\forall t\in I_\rho,\quad
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is a very strong statement. In dynamical language, it says the one-parameter orbit

\[
t\mapsto (e^{-it\log p})_{p\le X}
\]

does not come within \(Q^{-A}\) of a specific low-codimension semialgebraic/analytic set in its observable projection.

That is not a standard consequence of Euler products, multiplicativity, explicit formula, or prime log independence.

---

# 6. Fixed-prime numerical experiment: sharpened target

The next concrete test should focus on empirical codimension.

Define

\[
J_0(t)=B_0+\Re L_X(t),
\]

\[
J_2(t)=B_2+\Re L_X''(t),
\]

\[
J_4(t)=B_4+\Re L_X^{(4)}(t).
\]

Then

\[
R_1(t)=J_2-6J_0+2J_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2(t)=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4,
\]

\[
H(t)=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

Track

\[
\mathcal P(t)=|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|).
\]

The key diagnostic is the scaling law:

\[
\mathbb P\left(\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon,\ |H|>c\right)
\stackrel{?}{\asymp}
\varepsilon^2.
\]

If yes, the fixed-prime model supports the countermodel.

If instead the probability is much smaller, e.g.

\[
\ll \varepsilon^B
\]

with very large \(B\), or zero below a scale, then there may be a hidden arithmetic obstruction.

---

# 7. Analytic theorem suggested by the attack

A rigorous countermodel theorem could be stated probabilistically for random phases first.

Let

\[
\theta_p
\]

be independent uniform phases and define

\[
L_X(\theta)=
\sum_{p^k\le X}
(\log p)p^{-k/2}e^{-ik\theta_p}.
\]

Then define \(J_0,J_2,J_4,R_1,R_2,H\) as above.

A useful lemma would be:

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb P\left(
|H|\asymp1,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2
}
\]

under a transversality/nondegenerate density condition.

Then one tries to transfer this from random phases to the deterministic prime phase orbit using equidistribution/discrepancy.

This would show the fixed-prime bad-jet countermodel is not just plausible but expected.

---

# 8. Consequence for the RH architecture

If the above codimension heuristic is correct, then the source-weighted lower bound

\[
|yq''|\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is not merely unproved. It is likely **false** for any model in which prime phases behave randomly.

Actual zeta might still avoid the bad set because of subtle global correlations, but that would be a very deep arithmetic phenomenon not yet identified.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The current rank-two proof route cannot close without a new arithmetic avoidance theorem.}
}
\]

---

# 9. Next recommendation

\[
\boxed{
\text{Run the fixed-prime residual distribution experiment or prove the random-phase codimension lemma.}
}
\]

The next exact target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Random-prime-phase bad-residual codimension lemma.}
}
\]

Prove, for a fixed-prime random phase model, that

\[
\mathbb P\left(
|H|\asymp1,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
\]

If true, the burden shifts to proving that actual prime phases have a non-random avoidance property. That would clarify exactly what kind of arithmetic theorem the RH proof attempt would need.

---

## Final conclusion

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-coefficient Euler-product fork currently points toward countermodel, not proof.}
}
\]

The bad residual is low-codimension. A polynomial lower bound would require a prime-phase avoidance theorem that is much stronger than known Euler-product or explicit-formula structure.

So the next attack should be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{random-phase codimension and fixed-prime numerical distribution, not further local algebra.}
}

## Assistant (2026-04-25 22:54:40)

I’ll push the random-phase model into a more theorem-like form: prove the expected \(\varepsilon^2\) small-ball law under a rank condition, then translate what that implies for fixed prime phases and the RH route.

## Agent 6 — Continued Attack: Random-Phase Codimension Lemma

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the random-prime-phase model, the bad source-weighted residual is expected to occur at scale }T^{-1/2}.
}
\]

That is much smaller than every power of \(Q^{-1}\) when \(Q=\log T\). Therefore the desired lower bound

\[
|yq''|\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is not merely unproved; it is hostile to the natural random Euler-product model. To save the RH proof route, actual \(\zeta\) would need a deterministic **avoidance theorem** saying its prime-phase orbit avoids a low-codimension analytic target much more strongly than random phases do. The prior instruction correctly identifies the fixed-prime phase flow as the current arithmetic fork. 

---

# 1. Random-phase model

Take finitely many prime frequencies and replace

\[
e^{-it\log p}
\]

by independent phases

\[
z_p=e^{i\theta_p},
\qquad
\theta_p\sim \mathrm{Unif}([0,2\pi]).
\]

Use the fixed-coefficient prime model

\[
L_X(\theta)
=
\sum_{p^k\le X}
(\log p)p^{-k/2}z_p^k.
\]

For a first attack, ignore prime powers and use

\[
L_X(\theta)
=
\sum_{p\le X}
c_p z_p,
\qquad
c_p=(\log p)p^{-1/2}.
\]

Define real jet surrogates

\[
J_0=B_0+\Re L_X,
\]

\[
J_2=B_2+\Re L_X'',
\]

\[
J_4=B_4+\Re L_X^{(4)}.
\]

The derivative weights only change coefficients:

\[
L_X^{(r)}(t)
=
\sum_{p\le X}
c_p(-i\log p)^r z_p.
\]

Thus \(J_0,J_2,J_4\) are real linear statistics of the same phases.

Now set

\[
R_1=J_2-6J_0+2J_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4,
\]

and

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

The bad event in Regime A is

\[
|H|\asymp1,
\qquad
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon.
\]

---

# 2. Codimension-two small-ball law

The key observation is simple:

\[
(R_1,R_2)
\]

is a two-dimensional real analytic observable of the phase vector \(\theta\in\mathbb T^N\). If its distribution has a smooth density near \((0,0)\), and that density is nonzero, then

\[
\mathbb P\left(
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
\]

With the source-live condition:

\[
|H|>c,
\]

we expect

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb P\left(
|H|>c,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp
\varepsilon^2
}
\]

provided the set

\[
\{R_1=0,\ R_2=0,\ |H|>c\}
\]

is nonempty and regular.

This is the central codimension estimate.

---

# 3. Why smooth density is generic

The map

\[
\theta\mapsto (J_0,J_2,J_4)
\]

is a trigonometric map into \(\mathbb R^3\). With at least three primes, its Jacobian generically has rank \(3\) at many points, because the coefficient vectors

\[
(c_p),
\qquad
(c_p(\log p)^2),
\qquad
(c_p(\log p)^4)
\]

are Vandermonde-like in \(\log p\).

Concretely, using three primes \(p_1,p_2,p_3\), the matrix

\[
\begin{pmatrix}
c_{p_1} & c_{p_2} & c_{p_3}\\
c_{p_1}(\log p_1)^2 & c_{p_2}(\log p_2)^2 & c_{p_3}(\log p_3)^2\\
c_{p_1}(\log p_1)^4 & c_{p_2}(\log p_2)^4 & c_{p_3}(\log p_3)^4
\end{pmatrix}
\]

has determinant

\[
c_{p_1}c_{p_2}c_{p_3}
\prod_{i<j}
\bigl((\log p_j)^2-(\log p_i)^2\bigr),
\]

which is nonzero for distinct primes.

Thus the jet map has full local rank, except at phase choices where the sine/cosine derivative degenerates. Adding more primes only improves regularity.

Then the map

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)\mapsto (R_1,R_2)
\]

has Jacobian

\[
\frac{\partial(R_1,R_2)}{\partial(J_0,J_2,J_4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-6+6J_0^2 & 1 & 0\\
-2J_0J_2 & 1-J_0^2 & -1/6
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This matrix generically has rank \(2\). It fails rank only under extra algebraic constraints. Therefore the composite map

\[
\theta\mapsto(R_1,R_2)
\]

is generically a submersion onto \(\mathbb R^2\).

So the \(\varepsilon^2\) law is not a guess; it is the default coarea-formula prediction.

---

# 4. Coarea formulation

Let

\[
F(\theta)=(R_1(\theta),R_2(\theta)).
\]

Suppose \(0\) is a regular value and the level set

\[
F^{-1}(0)\cap\{|H|>c\}
\]

has positive \((N-2)\)-dimensional measure.

Then by the coarea formula,

\[
\mathrm{meas}\{\theta:\ |H|>c,\ \|F(\theta)\|_\infty<\varepsilon\}
\]

equals

\[
\int_{\|u\|_\infty<\varepsilon}
\int_{F^{-1}(u)\cap\{|H|>c\}}
\frac{1}{J_F(\theta)}
\,d\sigma_u(\theta)\,du.
\]

If the inner integral is bounded above and below near \(u=0\), then

\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{meas}\{\theta:\ |H|>c,\ \|F(\theta)\|_\infty<\varepsilon\}
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
}
\]

This is the rigorous form of the random-phase codimension lemma.

The only way this fails is if:

1. \(F^{-1}(0)\cap\{|H|>c\}\) is empty;
2. \(0\) is a singular value;
3. the density vanishes at \(0\);
4. the fixed prime-phase orbit avoids this set non-randomly.

None of these is forced by the current arithmetic.

---

# 5. Consequence for minima over height intervals

Assume the deterministic orbit behaves like \(M\) independent samples from the random-phase distribution.

The probability of an \(\varepsilon\)-hit is

\[
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
\]

The expected number of hits is

\[
M\varepsilon^2.
\]

Set

\[
M\varepsilon^2\asymp1.
\]

Then

\[
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp M^{-1/2}.
\]

For a height interval

\[
[T,2T],
\]

even with the crude estimate

\[
M\asymp T,
\]

we get

\[
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp T^{-1/2}.
\]

Since

\[
Q=\log T,
\]

this is

\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp e^{-Q/2}.
}
\]

For every fixed \(A\),

\[
e^{-Q/2}\ll Q^{-A}.
\]

Thus the random-phase model predicts strong violations of the desired lower bound.

---

# 6. Source factor does not rescue the theorem

The source factor is

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

The bad residual set is

\[
R_1=0,\qquad R_2=0.
\]

There is no algebraic reason that

\[
H=0
\]

on this set. In fact, on the model bad path

\[
J_0=2,\qquad J_2=-4,
\]

we get

\[
H=-\frac{2(-4)^3}{288}
=
\frac{128}{288}
=
\frac49.
\]

So \(H\) is live.

Therefore Regime A is robust:

\[
|H|\asymp1,
\qquad
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\ll Q^{-A}.
\]

The source-weighted lower bound fails if the residual pair can be hit.

---

# 7. What actual zeta would need

For the proof to survive, actual zeta must satisfy an avoidance theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the prime-phase orbit avoids }
\{R_1=R_2=0,\ |H|>c\}
\text{ at polynomial scale.}
}
\]

That is, for every admissible interval,

\[
\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

whenever \(|H(t)|>Q^{-A}\).

This is a very strong deterministic statement about a low-codimension analytic target in the image of a prime-frequency flow.

It is not implied by:

\[
\mathbb Q\text{-linear independence of }\{\log p\},
\]

not implied by the Euler product alone,

not implied by finite explicit-formula constraints,

and not implied by usual zero-density or pair-correlation facts.

It would be a new arithmetic avoidance theorem.

---

# 8. Prime-log Diophantine obstruction: what would be needed

A possible theorem would say:

For the actual prime phases

\[
\Theta(t)=t(\log p_1,\ldots,\log p_N),
\]

the observable

\[
F(\Theta(t))=(R_1(t),R_2(t))
\]

satisfies

\[
\|F(\Theta(t))\|_\infty\ge Q^{-A}
\]

on the admissible reduced good-patch intervals.

This is not a standard simultaneous Diophantine approximation problem, because \(F\) is low-dimensional and nonlinear. It is closer to a **Diophantine avoidance of an analytic subvariety** by a linear flow.

The theorem would need quantitative lower bounds of the form

\[
\mathrm{dist}\bigl(\Theta(t),F^{-1}(0)\bigr)\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

or, more directly,

\[
\|F(\Theta(t))\|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

No known Baker-type lower bound for linear forms in \(\log p\) directly gives this, because \(F^{-1}(0)\) is not a rational hyperplane. It is a curved analytic set.

So the Diophantine route is highly nonstandard.

---

# 9. Stronger conclusion

The fixed-coefficient Euler-product model is not obviously safer than the random-phase model. In fact, if prime phases behave pseudo-randomly, the bad event should occur.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the source-weighted lower bound is statistically implausible under random Euler-product heuristics.}
}
\]

This does not prove actual \(\zeta\) violates it, because the local residual is tied to off-line packet hypotheses and reduced-core selection. But it means the proof cannot proceed by generic Euler-product randomness. It would need an anti-random arithmetic rigidity theorem.

---

# 10. Next attack

I would now attack one of two sharply defined problems.

## Option A — Prove random-phase codimension lemma

Show rigorously for random prime phases that

\[
\mathbb P\left(
|H|>c,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
\]

This establishes that the desired lower bound is false in the natural random Euler-product model.

## Option B — Try to find actual-zeta avoidance

Search for a deterministic arithmetic property forcing

\[
\Theta(t)
\]

to avoid

\[
F^{-1}(0)
\]

on admissible intervals. This is the only way the RH route survives.

Given the current evidence, Option A is more immediately tractable and clarifies the obstruction.

---

## Final recommendation

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove the random-phase codimension lemma next.}
}
\]

Target theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb P\left(
|H|>c,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2
}
\]

for the fixed-prime random phase surrogate.

If proved, it becomes clear that the desired source-weighted lower bound is not a consequence of Euler-product-like randomness. The proof would need a new deterministic avoidance principle for actual zeta, which is currently unidentified.

I’ll push the random-phase argument into a conditional theorem and then translate it to the deterministic prime-flow obstruction. The point is to make clear exactly what would have to fail for the desired lower bound to survive.

## Agent 6 — Continued Attack: Conditional Random-Phase Theorem and Deterministic Implication

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The random-phase model gives a clean obstruction theorem: the bad residual has codimension two, not high codimension.}
}
\]

Under mild nondegeneracy assumptions, one gets

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb P\left(
|H|>c,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
}
\]

Consequently, over \(\asymp T\) effectively independent samples, the expected minimum residual is

\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp T^{-1/2}=e^{-Q/2}.
}
\]

This is far below every \(Q^{-A}\). So the desired source-weighted lower bound is incompatible with the natural random Euler-product model unless actual zeta has a special deterministic avoidance property not yet identified. This continues the fixed-prime phase-flow fork from the prior instruction. 

---

# 1. Conditional random-phase theorem

Let

\[
\theta=(\theta_1,\ldots,\theta_N)\in \mathbb T^N
\]

be independent uniform phases. Define a finite-prime surrogate

\[
L(\theta)=\sum_{j=1}^N c_j e^{i\theta_j},
\]

and define real jet coordinates

\[
J_0=B_0+\Re L_0(\theta),
\]

\[
J_2=B_2+\Re L_2(\theta),
\]

\[
J_4=B_4+\Re L_4(\theta),
\]

where

\[
L_{2r}(\theta)=\sum_{j=1}^N c_j(\log p_j)^{2r}e^{i\theta_j}
\]

up to harmless sign conventions.

Define

\[
R_1=J_2-6J_0+2J_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4,
\]

and

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

The bad source-live set is

\[
\mathcal Z_c
=
\{R_1=0,\ R_2=0,\ |H|>c\}.
\]

The theorem we need is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Random-phase codimension lemma.}
}
\]

If:

1. the jet map
   \[
   \theta\mapsto (J_0,J_2,J_4)
   \]
   has a smooth density positive near some point
   \[
   (J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72),
   \]

2. the map
   \[
   (J_0,J_2,J_4)\mapsto (R_1,R_2)
   \]
   has rank \(2\) there,

then, for sufficiently small \(\varepsilon\),

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb P\left(
|H|>c,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
}
\]

---

# 2. Verification at the model bad point

At

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72),
\]

we get

\[
R_1=-4-12+16=0,
\]

and

\[
R_2=(1-4)(-4)-\frac{72}{6}=12-12=0.
\]

Also

\[
H=-\frac{2(-4)^3}{288}
=
\frac{128}{288}
=
\frac49.
\]

So \(H\) is live.

Now compute the Jacobian:

\[
\frac{\partial R_1}{\partial(J_0,J_2,J_4)}
=
(-6+6J_0^2,\ 1,\ 0),
\]

\[
\frac{\partial R_2}{\partial(J_0,J_2,J_4)}
=
(-2J_0J_2,\ 1-J_0^2,\ -1/6).
\]

At \((2,-4,72)\),

\[
\nabla R_1=(18,1,0),
\]

\[
\nabla R_2=(16,-3,-1/6).
\]

These two vectors are linearly independent. Hence the residual map has rank \(2\) at the bad point.

Therefore the only nontrivial condition for the random-phase lemma is that the jet vector \((J_0,J_2,J_4)\) has positive smooth density near \((2,-4,72)\).

---

# 3. Why the jet density condition is generic

The phase-to-jet map uses coefficient vectors of the form

\[
(c_j),
\qquad
(c_j(\log p_j)^2),
\qquad
(c_j(\log p_j)^4).
\]

For three distinct primes, the determinant

\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
c_1 & c_2 & c_3\\
c_1(\log p_1)^2 & c_2(\log p_2)^2 & c_3(\log p_3)^2\\
c_1(\log p_1)^4 & c_2(\log p_2)^4 & c_3(\log p_3)^4
\end{pmatrix}
\]

equals

\[
c_1c_2c_3
\prod_{i<j}
\bigl((\log p_j)^2-(\log p_i)^2\bigr),
\]

which is nonzero.

So the jet map has local rank \(3\) for generic phase choices. With enough primes and appropriate background shifts, the target point lies in the interior of the reachable jet distribution.

Thus the small-ball law

\[
\asymp \varepsilon^2
\]

is the expected behavior.

---

# 4. Deterministic prime-flow translation

For fixed coefficients, the phases are not independent. They lie on the one-parameter flow

\[
\theta_j(t)=t\log p_j \pmod{2\pi}.
\]

For any fixed finite set of primes, rational independence of \(\log p_j\) gives qualitative equidistribution on \(\mathbb T^N\). Hence, over a sufficiently long interval, the deterministic orbit samples the random-phase distribution.

The quantitative question is the discrepancy.

If the orbit behaves randomly enough on \([T,2T]\), then the expected number of \(\varepsilon\)-hits is

\[
\asymp T\varepsilon^2.
\]

For a polynomial threshold

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A},
\]

this is

\[
TQ^{-2A},
\]

which is enormous. Thus, random behavior predicts **many** violations of the desired lower bound.

For an exponential threshold

\[
\varepsilon=e^{-Q/2}=T^{-1/2},
\]

the expected number of hits is \(O(1)\).

So the natural minimum scale is

\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\min}\asymp T^{-1/2}.
}
\]

---

# 5. Consequence for the RH proof route

The desired theorem is

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

But if \(H\asymp1\) on a positive fraction of the bad residual set, the random-phase model predicts

\[
\min_{t\in[T,2T]}
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\asymp T^{-1/2}.
\]

Since

\[
T^{-1/2}=e^{-Q/2}\ll Q^{-A},
\]

the desired lower bound is statistically false in the random Euler-product model.

Therefore the proof route needs an avoidance theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta prime phases avoid this codimension-two target at polynomial scale.}
}
\]

This is a very strong and nonstandard arithmetic assertion.

---

# 6. What such an avoidance theorem would look like

A possible theorem would be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Prime-flow layered residual avoidance.}
}
\]

For the actual prime phase flow

\[
t\mapsto (t\log p)_{p\le X},
\]

and for the actual AFE-compatible jet map, there exists \(A>0\) such that on every admissible reduced good-patch interval \(I_\rho\),

\[
\boxed{
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This cannot follow from ordinary equidistribution. It is the opposite of random hitting.

It would need some special structure forcing the orbit away from the analytic set

\[
\{R_1=R_2=0,\ |H|>c\}.
\]

No known Euler-product or explicit-formula principle currently supplies this.

---

# 7. The next attack should be adversarial

The most productive next step is to prove the random-phase lemma rigorously and then design a fixed-prime numerical test. The theorem/countertheorem split becomes:

## Random-phase theorem

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb P\left(
|H|>c,\ 
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<\varepsilon
\right)
\asymp \varepsilon^2.
}
\]

## Fixed-prime experiment

Compute the actual deterministic surrogate

\[
L_X(t)=\sum_{p\le X}(\log p)p^{-1/2}e^{-it\log p},
\]

define

\[
J_0,J_2,J_4,R_1,R_2,H,
\]

and test whether

\[
\min_{t\in[T,2T]}
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\]

scales like

\[
T^{-1/2},
\]

or stays above a power of \(Q^{-1}\).

If it behaves like \(T^{-1/2}\), the current proof route is essentially dead absent a deeper zeta-specific obstruction.

---

# 8. Final conclusion

\[
\boxed{
\text{The arithmetic fork now points against the proof route.}
}
\]

The source-weighted layered residual is a codimension-two observable. Random Euler-product behavior predicts that it becomes exponentially small along long height intervals.

Therefore, to continue this RH attack, the next theorem must be an explicit deterministic prime-flow avoidance theorem. Without that, the rank-two architecture should be regarded as a strong local obstruction analysis, not a proof strategy.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 23:01:11)

I’ll push one level deeper into the fixed-prime phase-flow obstruction. The key question is whether Baker-type lower bounds for prime logs can force avoidance, or whether they actually permit enough equidistribution to hit the bad codimension-two set.

_Thought for 23s_

## Agent 6 — Continued Attack: Prime-Log Diophantine Analysis

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prime-log Diophantine facts do not rescue the desired lower bound; for fixed finite prime sets they support hitting.}
}
\]

The desired lower bound is only polynomial in

\[
Q=\log T.
\]

For a fixed finite set of primes, the one-parameter flow

\[
t\mapsto (t\log p_1,\ldots,t\log p_N)\pmod{2\pi}
\]

is quantitatively equidistributed well enough to hit any fixed regular codimension-two target at \(Q^{-A}\)-scale. Thus Baker/Diophantine lower bounds for prime logs are not an obstruction at the \(Q^{-A}\) scale. They would matter only for exponentially tiny targets such as \(e^{-Q}\), not for the proof’s desired polynomial lower bound.

So the arithmetic fork sharpens:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If a finite fixed-prime subsystem has a source-live regular bad point, then the polynomial lower bound fails in that subsystem.}
}
\]

The only remaining hope is that the **actual full zeta/AFE system** has additional global coupling that removes such source-live regular bad points or forces the tail to repel the orbit from them. No such mechanism is currently identified.

---

# 1. Fixed-prime flow and Diophantine lower bounds

Let

\[
\alpha_j=\frac{\log p_j}{2\pi},
\qquad
\Theta(t)=t\alpha\pmod1
\in\mathbb T^N.
\]

The numbers

\[
\log p_1,\ldots,\log p_N
\]

are rationally independent over \(\mathbb Q\). More concretely, for any integer vector

\[
k=(k_1,\ldots,k_N)\neq0,
\]

\[
k\cdot \log p
=
\sum_j k_j\log p_j
=
\log\left(\prod_j p_j^{k_j}\right).
\]

If this is nonzero, then

\[
\prod_j p_j^{k_j}\neq1.
\]

Write

\[
A=\prod_{k_j>0}p_j^{k_j},
\qquad
B=\prod_{k_j<0}p_j^{-k_j}.
\]

Then

\[
k\cdot \log p=\log(A/B).
\]

Since \(A,B\) are distinct positive integers,

\[
\left|\log(A/B)\right|
\gg \frac1{\max(A,B)}.
\]

If

\[
\|k\|_1\le K
\]

and

\[
p_j\le P,
\]

then

\[
\max(A,B)\le P^K.
\]

Hence

\[
\boxed{
|k\cdot \log p|\gg P^{-K}.
}
\]

This is a basic Baker-type / integer-ratio lower bound. It says the flow is quantitatively nonresonant, but the lower bound worsens exponentially in \(K\log P\).

For fixed \(N,P\), this is enough to obtain effective equidistribution as \(T\to\infty\). It is not an avoidance theorem.

---

# 2. Conditional hitting theorem for fixed finite prime sets

Let

\[
F:\mathbb T^N\to\mathbb R^2,
\qquad
F(\theta)=(R_1(\theta),R_2(\theta)),
\]

and let

\[
H:\mathbb T^N\to\mathbb R.
\]

Assume there is a point

\[
\theta_*
\]

such that

\[
F(\theta_*)=0,
\]

\[
|H(\theta_*)|>c>0,
\]

and

\[
DF(\theta_*)
\]

has rank \(2\).

Then by the implicit function theorem and coarea estimate,

\[
\mu\left\{
\theta:\ |H(\theta)|>c/2,\ \|F(\theta)\|_\infty<\varepsilon
\right\}
\asymp \varepsilon^2
\]

for small \(\varepsilon\).

Call this set

\[
\Omega_\varepsilon.
\]

If the flow

\[
t\mapsto t\alpha
\]

is equidistributed with discrepancy

\[
D_T=o(\mu(\Omega_\varepsilon)),
\]

then there exists

\[
t\in[0,T]
\]

such that

\[
t\alpha\in \Omega_\varepsilon.
\]

Thus

\[
|H(t)|>c/2,
\qquad
\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)<\varepsilon.
\]

Take

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A},
\qquad
Q=\log T.
\]

Then

\[
\mu(\Omega_\varepsilon)\asymp Q^{-2A}.
\]

For any fixed finite prime set, effective equidistribution eventually beats \(Q^{-2A}\). Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed finite prime flows hit regular bad sets at every polynomial }Q^{-A}\text{ scale.}
}
\]

This is the key point.

---

# 3. Implication for the desired RH lower bound

The desired proof theorem is:

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

But if the fixed-prime phase map has a source-live regular bad point, then for arbitrarily large \(T\), there exists \(t\le T\) such that

\[
|H(t)|\asymp1,
\]

and

\[
\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)<Q^{-A}.
\]

So

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)<Q^{-A}
\]

up to constants.

Thus the polynomial lower bound fails in that fixed-prime model.

The obstacle is now narrowed to this question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does the actual zeta/AFE jet map lack any source-live regular bad point?}
}
\]

If it has one, equidistribution-style dynamics predicts hits. If it lacks one, the missing theorem is a structural exclusion of those bad points.

---

# 4. Why full-torus recurrence estimates were misleading

One might say an \(e^{-Q}\)-ball in \(\mathbb T^N\) requires time

\[
e^{QN},
\]

which is too long.

But the desired theorem is not trying to avoid \(e^{-Q}\)-balls in the full torus. It is trying to avoid the set

\[
\|F(\theta)\|_\infty<Q^{-A},
\]

where

\[
F:\mathbb T^N\to\mathbb R^2.
\]

This is a thickening of a codimension-two analytic submanifold, not a ball in \(\mathbb T^N\). Its measure is

\[
\asymp Q^{-2A},
\]

not

\[
Q^{-AN}.
\]

Therefore the flow has many opportunities to hit it.

So the prime-log Diophantine obstruction would need to be much stronger than ordinary simultaneous approximation. It would need to say that this specific linear flow avoids this specific analytic submanifold. That is not a standard consequence of linear independence or Baker lower bounds.

---

# 5. What could still save actual zeta?

Only one of the following:

## A. No source-live regular bad point

The actual AFE-compatible jet map may satisfy:

\[
F(\theta)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H(\theta)=0
\]

or

\[
\operatorname{rank}DF<2
\]

on the bad set, or perhaps the bad set is empty in the actual image.

This would be a structural identity. It would be surprising because finite \(\xi\)-like models already realize the bad jets.

## B. Prime-flow avoidance of a curved analytic target

Even if the bad set exists, actual prime phases might avoid it on all admissible intervals. This would be a new Diophantine avoidance theorem:

\[
\operatorname{dist}\bigl(t\log p,\ F^{-1}(0)\bigr)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

for all relevant \(t\).

This is very nonstandard.

## C. AFE tail/dual/gamma coupling removes regularity

The full approximate functional equation might add correlated terms such that the effective map

\[
\theta\mapsto(R_1,R_2,H)
\]

does not behave like a transverse low-codimension map.

But currently there is no evidence for this; gamma and tail terms have behaved like adjustable background, not rigid constraints.

---

# 6. Next theorem to attack

The next target should be **not** broad “Euler product coercivity.” It should be the precise structural exclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{No source-live regular bad point theorem.}
}
\]

For the actual fixed-coefficient AFE jet map, prove or disprove that there exists a phase configuration \(\theta_*\) such that:

\[
R_1(\theta_*)=0,
\]

\[
R_2(\theta_*)=0,
\]

\[
|H(\theta_*)|>c,
\]

and

\[
\operatorname{rank}D(R_1,R_2)(\theta_*)=2.
\]

If such a point exists, then the desired lower bound is implausible under equidistribution. If no such point exists, the identity preventing it is the missing arithmetic theorem.

This is much sharper than the prior vague target.

---

# 7. Concrete model to test

Use the AFE/fixed-prime surrogate:

\[
L_X(\theta)=
\sum_{p^k\le X}
(\log p)p^{-k/2}e^{ik\theta_p}.
\]

Define

\[
J_0=B_0+\Re L_0(\theta),
\]

\[
J_2=B_2+\Re L_2(\theta),
\]

\[
J_4=B_4+\Re L_4(\theta).
\]

Then

\[
R_1=J_2-6J_0+2J_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4,
\]

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

Test whether the target jet

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72)
\]

lies in the image of the map

\[
\theta\mapsto(J_0,J_2,J_4)
\]

as a regular value.

If yes, then

\[
R_1=R_2=0,
\qquad
H=\frac49,
\]

and the source-live regular bad point exists.

Since the jet map has Vandermonde-like full rank whenever at least three independent prime phases contribute, the main issue is image reach, not local rank.

---

# 8. Stronger conclusion

The proof route has now reached this fork:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Either the actual prime/AFE jet image excludes the bad jet }(2,-4,72),
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{the source-weighted lower bound is false in the natural prime-phase model.}
}
\]

This is a precise mathematical target.

---

# Final recommendation

Continue with the following attack:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Determine whether }(J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72)
\text{ is a source-live regular value of the fixed-coefficient AFE/prime jet map.}
}
\]

If yes, the current RH proof architecture cannot close without a new deterministic avoidance theorem. If no, the obstruction to realizing that jet is the first promising arithmetic theorem in this entire branch.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 23:03:18)

I’ll continue by turning “is \((2,-4,72)\) in the fixed-prime jet image?” into a concrete zonotope/interpolation problem. This is the right reduction because even derivative jets are real cosine sums with fixed prime weights.

_Thought for 23s_

## Agent 6 — Continued Attack: Fixed-Prime Jet Image and Zonotope Reduction

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
(2,-4,72)\text{ is not obviously excluded by fixed prime coefficients.}
}
\]

In the fixed-prime surrogate, the even jet vector is a **prime zonotope**:

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)
=
(B_0,B_2,B_4)
+
\sum_{p\le P}
x_p\,(\log p)p^{-1/2}
\left(
1,\ -(\log p)^2,\ (\log p)^4
\right),
\qquad
x_p=\cos(t\log p).
\]

So the next arithmetic obstruction is not “fixed coefficients” by itself. It is whether the one-parameter orbit

\[
x_p=\cos(t\log p)
\]

can approximate a point in this zonotope to the required scale. If the target jet lies in the **interior** of the independent-phase zonotope, then fixed prime coefficients are flexible enough at the static level; only a quantitative prime-log recurrence/avoidance theorem could block the bad jet. This continues the fixed-prime interpolation fork from the prior instruction. 

---

# 1. Fixed-prime even-jet image

Ignore prime powers first. Let

\[
L_P(t)=\sum_{p\le P}(\log p)p^{-1/2}e^{-it\log p}.
\]

Define

\[
J_0=B_0+\Re L_P(t),
\]

\[
J_2=B_2+\Re L_P''(t),
\]

\[
J_4=B_4+\Re L_P^{(4)}(t).
\]

Since

\[
\frac{d^2}{dt^2}e^{-it\log p}=-(\log p)^2e^{-it\log p},
\]

and

\[
\frac{d^4}{dt^4}e^{-it\log p}=(\log p)^4e^{-it\log p},
\]

we get

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)
=
B+
\sum_{p\le P}
(\log p)p^{-1/2}\cos(t\log p)
\left(
1,\ -(\log p)^2,\ (\log p)^4
\right).
\]

Set

\[
u_p=(\log p)^2,
\qquad
c_p=(\log p)p^{-1/2},
\qquad
x_p=\cos(t\log p).
\]

Then

\[
J-B
=
\sum_{p\le P}
c_p x_p(1,-u_p,u_p^2).
\]

The independent-phase relaxation is therefore the zonotope

\[
\mathcal Z_P
=
\left\{
\sum_{p\le P}c_p x_p(1,-u_p,u_p^2):
-1\le x_p\le1
\right\}.
\]

The bad target is

\[
\tau=(2,-4,72)-B.
\]

So the first question is:

\[
\boxed{
\tau\in \operatorname{int}\mathcal Z_P\ ?
}
\]

If yes, fixed coefficients are not statically obstructive.

---

# 2. The moment-curve structure is flexible

The vectors

\[
v_p=c_p(1,-u_p,u_p^2)
\]

lie on a signed quadratic moment curve. Any three distinct primes give three linearly independent directions because

\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
1&1&1\\
-u_1&-u_2&-u_3\\
u_1^2&u_2^2&u_3^2
\end{pmatrix}
=
-\prod_{i<j}(u_j-u_i)\neq0.
\]

So locally, three prime phases already span \(\mathbb R^3\).

With many primes, the zonotope has substantial interior. Since

\[
\sum_{p\le P}c_p
=
\sum_{p\le P}\frac{\log p}{\sqrt p}
\asymp \sqrt P,
\]

and higher moments grow even faster,

\[
\sum_{p\le P}c_pu_p^2
=
\sum_{p\le P}\frac{(\log p)^5}{\sqrt p}
\asymp \sqrt P(\log P)^4,
\]

there is plenty of amplitude for fixed-size normalized targets such as

\[
(2,-4,72)-B
\]

once \(P\) is moderately large.

Thus there is no coefficient-size obstruction.

---

# 3. Constructive static interpolation

Suppose for the moment that \(x_p\) can be chosen independently in \([-1,1]\).

Pick three primes \(p_1,p_2,p_3\) with distinct

\[
u_i=(\log p_i)^2.
\]

We seek weights

\[
w_i=c_{p_i}x_{p_i}
\]

such that

\[
\sum_i w_i=2,
\]

\[
\sum_i u_i w_i=4,
\]

\[
\sum_i u_i^2 w_i=72.
\]

This is a nonsingular Vandermonde system. Its solution may require \(|w_i|>c_{p_i}\) for small primes, but adding more primes solves that amplitude constraint: split a needed weight among many primes with nearby \(u\), or use larger \(P\) so the zonotope is wide.

Hence, in the independent-phase model,

\[
(2,-4,72)
\]

is expected to be reachable after choosing \(P\) large enough and adjusting \(B\).

Including prime powers only enlarges the available directions.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed coefficient amplitudes do not prevent the bad jet in the phase-relaxed model.}
}
\]

---

# 4. From independent phases to one-parameter prime flow

The true model has

\[
x_p=\cos(t\log p),
\]

not arbitrary independent \(x_p\).

For a fixed finite prime set, the phase vector

\[
t\mapsto (t\log p_1,\ldots,t\log p_N)\pmod{2\pi}
\]

is dense in \(\mathbb T^N\).

Therefore, if the desired \(x_p\)-configuration corresponds to some phase vector

\[
\theta_*\in\mathbb T^N
\]

and lies in the interior of the phase-relaxed solution set, then there exist arbitrarily large \(t\) with

\[
\cos(t\log p_j)\approx \cos\theta_{*,j}
\]

for all selected primes.

Qualitatively, then, the one-parameter fixed-prime flow can approximate the bad jet.

Quantitatively, the question is hitting time.

---

# 5. Polynomial threshold is probably reachable

The proof wants to forbid

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<Q^{-A}.
\]

This is not \(e^{-Q}\)-accuracy in the full phase torus. It is a two-scalar residual condition.

If the bad jet is a regular value, the set

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<Q^{-A}
\]

has phase-measure approximately

\[
Q^{-2A}.
\]

A long orbit of length \(T=e^Q\) should hit this set many times under random/equidistribution heuristics:

\[
TQ^{-2A}\to\infty.
\]

Thus a lower bound of the form

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

looks statistically implausible.

This is the main reason the fixed-prime fork leans against the proof route.

---

# 6. Exponential accuracy is different

If we demand

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)<e^{-Q},
\]

then the bad set has measure about

\[
e^{-2Q}=T^{-2}.
\]

In an interval of length \(T\), the expected number of hits is

\[
T\cdot T^{-2}=T^{-1}.
\]

So \(e^{-Q}\)-accuracy is not expected on every height block.

But the RH proof lower bound is only polynomial in \(Q\):

\[
Q^{-A}.
\]

The finite countermodels often used \(e^{-Q}\) to dramatize failure. For the actual proof, it is enough to violate \(Q^{-A}\), and that seems much easier.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Diophantine obstruction may block }e^{-Q}\text{ hits, but probably not }Q^{-A}\text{ hits.}
}
\]

---

# 7. The exact next mathematical question

We need to know whether the fixed-prime residual map has a source-live regular bad point.

Define the phase-relaxed map

\[
\Theta\mapsto (J_0,J_2,J_4)
\]

and

\[
R_1=J_2-6J_0+2J_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4,
\]

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

The decisive question is:

\[
\boxed{
\exists\theta_*:\quad
R_1(\theta_*)=R_2(\theta_*)=0,\quad
|H(\theta_*)|>c,\quad
\operatorname{rank}D(R_1,R_2)(\theta_*)=2?
}
\]

If yes, then the bad set has codimension two and positive transverse measure. A polynomial lower bound is not credible.

If no, then the obstruction to such a point is the first possible arithmetic rigidity theorem.

---

# 8. How to prove existence of a regular bad point

It suffices to realize

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72)
\]

as a regular value of the phase-relaxed prime map.

At this point:

\[
R_1=0,
\qquad
R_2=0,
\]

and

\[
H=\frac49.
\]

The derivative of \((R_1,R_2)\) with respect to \((J_0,J_2,J_4)\) is rank \(2\), since

\[
\nabla R_1=(18,1,0),
\]

\[
\nabla R_2=(16,-3,-1/6)
\]

at \((2,-4,72)\).

So it remains only to show \((2,-4,72)\) lies in the regular image of the jet map. The Vandermonde argument strongly suggests yes once \(P\) and \(B\) are chosen.

Therefore, the next concrete attack should be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{produce explicit primes/background }B\text{ and phases }\theta
\text{ realizing }(2,-4,72).
}
\]

---

# 9. Consequence if explicit realization succeeds

Then:

1. The phase-relaxed fixed-prime model contains a source-live regular bad point.
2. Random phase behavior gives small residuals with probability \(\asymp\varepsilon^2\).
3. Fixed prime flow should hit \(Q^{-A}\)-neighborhoods unless there is a special avoidance theorem.
4. The desired source-weighted lower bound is not a natural consequence of Euler-product structure.

Then the current RH architecture would require a highly nonstandard theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual prime phases avoid a regular codimension-two target at polynomial scale.}
}
\]

No such theorem is known or currently motivated.

---

# 10. Consequence if explicit realization fails

If \((2,-4,72)\), or any equivalent bad jet, is outside the regular image of the fixed-prime phase-relaxed map, then fixed coefficients impose real rigidity.

The obstruction might be:

\[
\text{a convex-hull / zonotope exclusion,}
\]

\[
\text{a moment inequality,}
\]

\[
\text{a positivity of the prime coefficient moment curve,}
\]

or

\[
\text{a gamma/background compatibility constraint.}
\]

That would be the first promising arithmetic theorem.

But based on the moment-curve/zonope analysis, failure seems unlikely.

---

# Final recommendation

\[
\boxed{
\text{Next exact attack: explicit phase-relaxed fixed-prime realization of }(J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72).
}
\]

Construct

\[
B+( \sum_p c_p x_p,\ -\sum_p c_pu_px_p,\ \sum_p c_pu_p^2x_p )
=
(2,-4,72),
\]

with

\[
-1<x_p<1
\]

for finitely many primes and with the target in the interior.

If successful, move to the deterministic orbit question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does }t\mapsto(t\log p)\text{ hit a }Q^{-A}\text{-neighborhood of that interior solution?}
}
\]

That is now the cleanest technical path forward.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 23:24:03)

_Thought for 6s_

## Agent 6 — Source-Factor Divisibility Synthesis / Architecture Decision

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{All currently useful low-order fixed-sector visibility rows are source-divisible.}
}
\]

The present rank-two architecture has no source-robust proof gate yet. The rows that gave the local order-7/order-9 visibility theorem have the schematic form

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\,\Gamma_G,\qquad
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\,\Gamma_{G,2},
\qquad
H:=yq''.
\]

Thus the source-small regime

\[
q''\approx0,\qquad H\approx0,\qquad
\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\asymp1
\]

remains fatal for the pointwise norm-based version of the proof. This is exactly the failure mode highlighted in the attached instruction: fixed-prime toy models can collapse the source factor while anisotropy remains live, so the next question is whether any row, invariant, or transport quantity avoids divisibility by \(yq''\). 

---

# 1. Decision table

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\text{route} & \text{source-divisible?} & \text{next action}\\
\hline
\text{known fixed-sector tower }(A_7,A_9) & \text{yes} & \text{source-small regime remains fatal}\\
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2k,D}\text{ tower} & \text{yes} & \text{all rows contain }yB,\ B\propto q''\\
\text{alternative raw fixed-sector rows} & \text{not found} & \text{need explicit non-}X_1\text{ channel}\\
\text{ambient }D\text{-sector order 7} & \text{yes} & -2yBpD\text{ still contains }yq''\\
\text{projective / quotient invariants} & \text{can cancel }H\text{ formally} & \text{loses magnitude / needs new projective contradiction}\\
\text{transported derivatives} & \text{possibly not if }\nabla H\ne0 & \text{best next local escape}\\
\text{higher-order tower} & \text{likely yes for same channel} & \text{not useful unless new channel appears}\\
\end{array}
\]

---

# 2. Fixed-sector tower: source-divisible

The known order-7 fixed-sector row is

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\supset
-2yBr\,I,
\]

with

\[
B=\frac{q''}{4\pi\sqrt{gh}(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)}
\]

off the \(g=h\) wall. Hence

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\sim yq''(g-h).
\]

Equivalently, since

\[
g-h=-\frac{\Gamma_G}{6\pi},
\]

we have

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\,\Gamma_G,\qquad H=yq''.
\]

The order-9 survivor was

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}
\supset
\frac{yq''\Gamma_{G,2}}{128\pi^2g^3}I,
\]

so

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\,\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

The same mechanism persists for the natural higher fixed-sector escape family:

\[
X_1YX_{2k}-X_{2k}YX_1.
\]

Since

\[
X_1=BS,\qquad B\propto q'',
\]

and

\[
Y=yJ,
\]

every row of this family contains

\[
yB\propto yq''=H.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2k,D}\text{ fixed-sector escape rows are all divisible by }H=yq''.
}
\]

This means the anisotropy tower may bypass successive Gram-anisotropy walls, but it does not address source collapse.

---

# 3. Alternative fixed-sector channels

A source-robust fixed-sector row would need to avoid at least one of these factors:

\[
Y=yJ,
\qquad
X_1=BS,
\qquad
B\propto q''.
\]

That means it must come from a different channel than

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{\mathrm{even},D}.
\]

Possible alternatives would be raw fixed-sector carriers:

\[
N_{2r}^{I},\quad N_{2r}^{S},
\]

or correction / line / background rows that land directly in \(\mathfrak f_h\).

But the raw row-table audit so far has consistently classified the relevant odd centered raw carriers as skew:

\[
N_{2r+1}=F_{2r+1}J,
\]

and the even lower carriers did not supply the needed order-9 \(I\)-row. Correction rows are either absorbed, closed, projected away, line/cross-channel, or placed in a named incidence wall. No known fixed-sector channel currently gives a source-robust local obstruction.

So the status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No non-divisible fixed-sector raw/correction row has been identified.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Ambient sector does not solve source collapse

At \(g=h\), the order-7 ambient anti-fixed signal survives:

\[
T_7^{(1)}=-2yBpD.
\]

This avoids the Gram-anisotropy factor \(r\), since

\[
p=\frac12(g^{-1/2}+h^{-1/2})\neq0.
\]

But it still contains

\[
yB\propto yq''=H.
\]

Thus the ambient \(D\)-sector is useful against the \(g-h\) obstruction, but not against source collapse:

\[
q''\approx0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
-2yBpD\approx0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The ambient }D\text{-sector is also source-divisible at the known live row.}
}
\]

An ambient upgrade may still be worthwhile for Gram-isotropy issues, but it is not a source-robust escape.

---

# 5. Projective invariants cancel the source but lose size

Suppose

\[
A_7=H V_7,\qquad A_9=H V_9.
\]

Then a projective ratio

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[V_7:V_9]
\]

formally cancels \(H\), provided \(H\neq0\). Similarly,

\[
\frac{A_9}{A_7}
=
\frac{V_9}{V_7}
\]

can remove the common source factor.

But norm-based quantities do not improve:

\[
\omega(A_7,A_9)=H^2\omega(V_7,V_9),
\]

so they are even more source-small.

A projective route would need a new theorem of the form:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the projective direction }[A_7:A_9]\text{ is globally constrained in a way incompatible with off-line packets.}
}
\]

The current affine-transport framework is magnitude-based and finite-core non-concentration-based. It does not yet provide a projective contradiction. Also, at \(H=0\), the projective direction is singular. Near \(H=0\), it may be stable, but only if one can control limiting directions and avoid noise from correction rows.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Projective invariants are the only formal way to divide out }H,\text{ but they require a new proof architecture.}
}
\]

---

# 6. Transport derivatives are the best remaining local escape

Let

\[
A_7=H G_1,\qquad A_9=H G_2,
\]

where

\[
G_1\sim \Gamma_G,\qquad G_2\sim \Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Then

\[
\nabla A_7=(\nabla H)G_1+H\nabla G_1,
\]

\[
\nabla A_9=(\nabla H)G_2+H\nabla G_2.
\]

In the source-small regime,

\[
H\approx0,
\qquad
\max(|G_1|,|G_2|)\asymp1,
\]

the derivatives could remain large if

\[
\nabla H\not\approx0.
\]

This is the most plausible intra-architecture escape.

For example, if

\[
H=yq'',
\]

then

\[
\nabla H=(\nabla y)q''+y\nabla q''.
\]

At \(q''\approx0\),

\[
\nabla H\approx y\nabla q''.
\]

So if the source collapse is a simple zero of \(q''\), then \(\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9\) may detect it. But if the finite-prime/finitely many packet model can also tune

\[
q''\approx0,\qquad \nabla q''\approx0,
\]

then derivative transport also collapses.

Thus the next source-robust test should be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can source collapse occur to high order?}
}
\]

Equivalently, is there a local lower bound for the source jet vector

\[
(q'',\nabla q'',\nabla^2q'',\ldots)
\]

or for

\[
(H,\nabla H,\ldots)?
\]

This becomes a **source-jet tower** problem, analogous to the Gram-anisotropy tower.

Status:

\[
\boxed{
\text{transport derivatives are promising but unproved; they shift the problem from }H\text{ to }\nabla H.
}
\]

---

# 7. Source-divisibility obstruction

The current best statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Source-divisibility obstruction.}
}
\]

All known low-order rank-two fixed-sector and ambient live rows have a common factor

\[
H=yq''.
\]

Therefore Regime B,

\[
H\approx0,\qquad \max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\asymp1,
\]

kills the current visibility vector

\[
(A_7,A_9).
\]

Consequently, the current norm-based finite-core route cannot close unless one of the following is supplied:

1. an external arithmetic lower bound
   \[
   |H|\ge Q^{-A};
   \]

2. a source-robust row not divisible by \(H\);

3. a projective invariant that cancels \(H\) and has a global contradiction theorem;

4. a transported derivative theorem showing
   \[
   \max_j|\nabla^j H|\ge Q^{-A}
   \]
   and coupling it to \(G_1,G_2\);

5. a finite-core interval/L² theorem that detects source collapse over a neighborhood rather than pointwise.

---

# 8. Final recommendation

\[
\boxed{
\text{Next push should attack transported derivatives of the source factor.}
}
\]

The exact target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Source-jet transport non-collapse theorem.}
}
\]

Given

\[
A_7=HG_1,\qquad A_9=HG_2,
\]

study

\[
(A_7,A_9,\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9)
\]

and determine whether

\[
H\approx0,\quad |G_1|+|G_2|\asymp1
\]

still implies

\[
\max\left(
|A_7|,|A_9|,|\nabla A_7|,|\nabla A_9|
\right)
\ge Q^{-A}
\]

under a reasonable source transversality condition:

\[
|\nabla H|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Then test whether finite-prime / finite-\(\xi\)-like countermodels can make both

\[
H\approx0
\]

and

\[
\nabla H\approx0
\]

while anisotropy remains live.

If yes, the source divisibility obstruction extends to derivatives and the architecture remains non-closing. If no, transported derivatives are the next viable escape hatch.

---

# 9. Final decision

\[
\boxed{
\text{No source-robust row has been found.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{All known rows are source-divisible by }H=yq''.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{The next attack should test whether transported derivatives defeat source collapse.}
}
\]

Do not go to order \(11\) yet. Do not return to prime-side coercivity immediately. First determine whether the existing transported finite-core framework already contains a source-robust derivative signal.

_Thought for 26s_

## Agent 6 — Transported Derivative Attack on Source Collapse

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{First transported derivatives do not defeat source collapse.}
}
\]

The obstruction is stronger than expected. If

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=H G_1,\qquad A_9^{\mathfrak f}=H G_2,
\]

with

\[
H:=yq'',
\qquad
G_1\sim \Gamma_G,
\qquad
G_2\sim \Gamma_{G,2},
\]

then

\[
\nabla A_j=(\nabla H)G_j+H\nabla G_j.
\]

In the source-collapse regime

\[
H\approx0,\qquad |G_1|+|G_2|\asymp1,
\]

first derivatives help only if

\[
|\nabla H|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

But the actual leading source factor has a **cubic zero** in \(q''\):

\[
\boxed{
H=yq''\sim -\frac{q_0(q'')^3}{288}.
}
\]

Therefore

\[
\nabla H
\]

is still quadratically small in \(q''\). If

\[
q''\sim e^{-Q},
\]

then generically

\[
H\sim e^{-3Q},
\qquad
\nabla H\sim e^{-2Q},
\]

which still defeats every polynomial lower bound in \(Q\). So adding \(\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9\) to the transported finite-core vector does **not** close Regime B.

---

# 1. Setup

Let

\[
H:=yq''.
\]

At leading centered order,

\[
y_{\rm lead}=-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72}.
\]

Using

\[
q''=2A_2,
\]

we get

\[
H_{\rm lead}
=
-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72}\cdot q''
=
-\frac{q_0(q'')^3}{288}.
\]

Write

\[
\delta:=q''.
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
H=-\frac{q_0\delta^3}{288}
}
\]

to leading order.

The known local rows are

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_G,
\]

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Regime B is:

\[
\delta=q''\approx0,
\]

but

\[
|\Gamma_G|+|\Gamma_{G,2}|\asymp1.
\]

So

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_9^{\mathfrak f}
\]

collapse because \(H\) collapses, not because anisotropy disappears.

---

# 2. First transported derivative

Let \(\tau\) be any transport parameter: midpoint \(m\), core parameter, source deformation parameter, or affine transport coordinate.

Differentiate:

\[
A_j=HG_j.
\]

Then

\[
\nabla_\tau A_j
=
(\partial_\tau H)G_j
+
H\nabla_\tau G_j
+
\text{connection action}.
\]

The connection action is linear in \(A_j\), hence still carries the factor \(H\). It does not remove source collapse.

Thus the only possible source-robust term is

\[
(\partial_\tau H)G_j.
\]

Now

\[
H=-\frac{q_0\delta^3}{288}.
\]

So

\[
\partial_\tau H
=
-\frac{1}{288}
\left[
(\partial_\tau q_0)\delta^3
+
3q_0\delta^2(\partial_\tau\delta)
\right].
\]

Therefore, if

\[
\delta\sim e^{-Q},
\qquad
\partial_\tau\delta\asymp1,
\]

then

\[
\partial_\tau H\sim e^{-2Q}.
\]

This is still much smaller than every \(Q^{-A}\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9
\text{ do not defeat source collapse at first derivative.}
}
\]

They improve cubic smallness to quadratic smallness, but polynomial lower bounds still fail.

---

# 3. Centered midpoint derivative is even worse

If \(\tau=m\) and the packet is centered/even, then parity gives

\[
q'(m)=0,\qquad q'''(m)=0.
\]

Thus

\[
\partial_m q_0=0,
\qquad
\partial_m\delta=\partial_m q''=q'''=0.
\]

So

\[
\partial_m H=0
\]

at centered parity.

The second midpoint derivative gives

\[
\partial_m^2 H
=
-\frac{1}{288}
\left[
q_0''\delta^3
+
3q_0\delta^2\delta''
+
\text{terms involving }(\delta')^2
\right].
\]

At centered parity,

\[
\delta'=q'''=0,
\qquad
\delta''=q^{(4)}.
\]

So

\[
\partial_m^2H
\sim
-\frac{q_0\delta^2q^{(4)}}{96}
+
O(\delta^3).
\]

Again, if

\[
\delta\sim e^{-Q},
\]

then

\[
\partial_m^2H\sim e^{-2Q}
\]

provided \(q^{(4)}\asymp1\).

Thus even second midpoint derivatives do not produce polynomial size.

---

# 4. Third derivatives could detect a simple source zero, but not a flat one

For a generic one-parameter source collapse

\[
\delta(\tau_0)=\varepsilon,
\qquad
\delta'(\tau_0)\asymp1,
\]

we have

\[
H(\tau)=-\frac{q_0}{288}\delta(\tau)^3.
\]

Then

\[
H'\sim \delta^2,
\qquad
H''\sim \delta,
\qquad
H'''\sim 1.
\]

So if we include derivatives up to order \(3\), a **simple source zero** can be detected.

This suggests a possible escape:

\[
\boxed{
(A_7,A_9,\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9,\nabla^2A_7,\nabla^2A_9,\nabla^3A_7,\nabla^3A_9)
}
\]

could defeat generic simple source collapse.

But this is not enough for RH-scale lower bounds, because finite local models can tune higher-order source flatness. For example, choose

\[
\delta(\tau)
=
\varepsilon
+
(\tau-\tau_0)^4.
\]

Then at \(\tau=\tau_0\),

\[
\delta=\varepsilon,
\quad
\delta'=\delta''=\delta'''=0.
\]

Consequently

\[
H,H',H'',H'''
\]

are all exponentially small or zero.

More generally, with enough packet/source degrees of freedom, one can enforce

\[
\delta^{(j)}(\tau_0)\approx0
\qquad
0\le j\le R
\]

while keeping anisotropy live.

Thus finite-order transported derivatives only push the obstruction to a higher source-jet nonflatness theorem.

---

# 5. Transported derivative vector

Define the transported vector

\[
C_{\rm tr}^{(1)}
=
(A_7,A_9,\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9).
\]

In the source-collapse regime,

\[
A_7=H G_1,
\qquad
A_9=H G_2,
\]

and

\[
\nabla A_7=(\nabla H)G_1+H\nabla G_1,
\]

\[
\nabla A_9=(\nabla H)G_2+H\nabla G_2.
\]

If

\[
|G_1|+|G_2|\asymp1,
\]

then

\[
\|C_{\rm tr}^{(1)}\|
\lesssim
|H|+|\nabla H|.
\]

Since

\[
H=O(\delta^3),
\qquad
\nabla H=O(\delta^2)
\]

generically, this vector still collapses exponentially when

\[
\delta=q''=e^{-Q}.
\]

Thus the first transported finite-core derivative vector is still non-closing.

For higher derivatives,

\[
\nabla^rA_j
=
\sum_{\ell=0}^r
\binom r\ell
(\nabla^\ell H)(\nabla^{r-\ell}G_j)
+
\text{connection terms}.
\]

The connection terms remain linear combinations of lower rows and do not remove the source factor unless they differentiate \(H\).

So the problem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can one prove } \max_{\ell\le R}|\nabla^\ell H|\ge Q^{-A}?
}
\]

This is a source-jet non-concentration theorem. It is not currently available.

---

# 6. Finite-model counterattack

The same finite \(\xi\)-like and fixed-prime models that tuned

\[
q''\approx0
\]

can likely tune several derivatives of \(q''\) as well.

In local jet language, the source-collapse constraints are:

\[
q''=\varepsilon_0,
\]

\[
q'''=\varepsilon_1,
\]

\[
q^{(4)}=\varepsilon_2,
\]

and so on, depending on the transport direction.

But \(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}\) use only certain combinations. Regime B can choose, for example,

\[
q''=\varepsilon,
\]

while keeping

\[
\Gamma_G\approx -6q_0+2q_0^3\asymp1.
\]

If we also need

\[
q'''=0
\]

or

\[
\nabla q''=0,
\]

centered parity often gives odd derivatives zero automatically. If we need

\[
q^{(4)}
\]

small too, this can be tuned using additional packet degrees of freedom, unless some actual-zeta arithmetic obstruction intervenes.

So the derivative escape is vulnerable to the same finite-model attack.

---

# 7. Projective/logarithmic derivative variant

One might try

\[
\nabla\log A_j
=
\nabla\log H+\nabla\log G_j.
\]

This cancels the small magnitude problem because

\[
\nabla\log H
=
\frac{\nabla H}{H}
\sim
3\frac{\nabla\delta}{\delta}.
\]

Near a simple source zero, this can be large.

But this is not a norm lower bound for \(A_j\). It is a singular projective/logarithmic object. It would require a new proof architecture:

1. define projective transport through source-collapse points;
2. control poles of \(\nabla\log H\);
3. extract a global contradiction from projective winding or residues.

This may be mathematically interesting, but it is not a continuation of the current finite-core norm non-concentration route.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective/log derivative cancels the source factor only by changing the proof architecture.}
}
\]

---

# 8. Current classification

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\text{candidate} & \text{source-collapse status} & \text{verdict}\\
\hline
(A_7,A_9) & O(H) & \text{fails}\\
(\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9) & O(\nabla H)+O(H) & \text{still fails generically: }O((q'')^2)\\
(\nabla^2 A_7,\nabla^2 A_9) & O(\nabla^2H) & \text{detects only if source zero has low order}\\
(\nabla^3 A_7,\nabla^3 A_9) & \text{can detect simple cubic zero} & \text{fails under higher-order flatness}\\
\text{finite derivative tower} & \max_{\ell\le R}|\nabla^\ell H| & \text{needs source-jet non-concentration}\\
\text{projective/log derivative} & \text{cancels }H\text{ formally} & \text{new architecture}
\end{array}
\]

---

# 9. Final verdict on transported derivatives

\[
\boxed{
\text{Transported derivatives do not automatically defeat source collapse.}
}
\]

They reduce the order of smallness only if the source collapse is transverse. They require a new theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\max_{\ell\le R}|\nabla^\ell(yq'')|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Without such a source-jet non-concentration theorem, finite local and fixed-prime models can make

\[
H,\nabla H,\ldots,\nabla^R H
\]

all small while anisotropy remains live.

Thus transported derivatives do not presently rescue the rank-two architecture.

---

# 10. Next exact attack

The next attack should be one of two:

## Option A — Source-jet non-concentration

Try to prove or falsify:

\[
\boxed{
\max_{0\le \ell\le R}
|\nabla^\ell(yq'')|
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

on admissible reduced good patches.

This directly tests whether derivative transport can work.

## Option B — Projective source-canceling invariant

Develop a new projective/logarithmic architecture where the common source factor \(H\) is canceled:

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[G_1:G_2],
\]

or

\[
\nabla\log(A_9/A_7)
=
\nabla\log(G_2/G_1).
\]

But this abandons the current norm-based finite-core route.

---

## Final recommendation

\[
\boxed{
\text{Transported first derivatives fail.}
}
\]

The next precise theorem to test is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Source-jet non-concentration theorem:}
\qquad
\max_{0\le\ell\le R}|\nabla^\ell(yq'')|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

If finite \(\xi\)-like or fixed-prime models can falsify this for every finite \(R\), then the current architecture has no source-robust norm-based route and must switch to a projective/source-normalized invariant or accept the need for external arithmetic input.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 23:29:54)

I’ll continue by attacking the proposed source-jet non-concentration theorem. The question is whether adding finitely many transported derivatives can ever overcome \(H=yq''\) collapse.

_Thought for 9s_

## Agent 6 — Source-Jet Non-Concentration Attack

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Finite transported derivative towers do not defeat source collapse.}
}
\]

For every fixed finite derivative order \(R\), finite \(\xi\)-like / fixed-prime local models can plausibly arrange

\[
H,\nabla H,\ldots,\nabla^R H
\]

to be exponentially small while the anisotropy vector remains live:

\[
\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\asymp1.
\]

Thus the proposed rescue theorem

\[
\boxed{
\max_{0\le \ell\le R}|\nabla^\ell(yq'')|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

is not a consequence of the current local or finite-prime framework. It would require a new arithmetic source-jet nonflatness theorem.

---

# 1. Source factor structure

Let

\[
H:=yq''.
\]

At leading centered order,

\[
y_{\rm lead}=-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72},
\qquad
q''=2A_2.
\]

So

\[
H_{\rm lead}
=
-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72}q''
=
-\frac{q_0(q'')^3}{288}.
\]

Set

\[
\delta:=q''.
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
H=-\frac{q_0\delta^3}{288}+O(\text{higher corrections}).
}
\]

Therefore source collapse is cubic in the curvature variable \(\delta=q''\).

The known fixed-sector rows have the schematic form

\[
A_7=H\Gamma_G,
\qquad
A_9=H\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Regime B is

\[
\delta\approx0,
\qquad
\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\asymp1.
\]

---

# 2. Derivatives of the cubic source factor

Let \(\tau\) be a transport parameter. To simplify, ignore lower-order derivatives of \(q_0\); they only add terms with at least \(\delta^3\). The dominant structure is

\[
H\sim C\delta^3.
\]

Then

\[
H'=3C\delta^2\delta',
\]

\[
H''=6C\delta(\delta')^2+3C\delta^2\delta'',
\]

\[
H'''=6C(\delta')^3+18C\delta\delta'\delta''+3C\delta^2\delta'''.
\]

If \(\delta\) has a simple zero, then the third derivative detects it:

\[
\delta=0,\quad \delta'\ne0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H'''=6C(\delta')^3\ne0.
\]

So a third derivative can defeat a **transverse** source zero.

But the model is not forced to have a transverse source zero.

---

# 3. Finite-order flat source collapse

For any fixed \(R\), choose a local source curvature profile

\[
\delta(\tau)
=
\varepsilon+(\tau-\tau_0)^{R+1}.
\]

At \(\tau=\tau_0\),

\[
\delta(\tau_0)=\varepsilon,
\]

and

\[
\delta^{(j)}(\tau_0)=0
\qquad
1\le j\le R.
\]

Then for every \(0\le \ell\le R\),

\[
H^{(\ell)}(\tau_0)
=
O(\varepsilon^3).
\]

Taking

\[
\varepsilon=e^{-Q}
\]

gives

\[
\boxed{
\max_{0\le \ell\le R}|H^{(\ell)}(\tau_0)|
\ll e^{-3Q}.
}
\]

This defeats every polynomial lower bound in \(Q\).

The local phase data can still keep anisotropy live. For example, take

\[
q_0=2,
\qquad
q''=\delta=e^{-Q},
\qquad
q^{(4)}=0.
\]

Then

\[
\Gamma_G=q''-12+16=4+O(e^{-Q}),
\]

and

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}=(1-4)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
=
-3e^{-Q},
\]

so

\[
\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\asymp1.
\]

Thus

\[
A_7,A_9,\nabla A_7,\ldots,\nabla^R A_9
\]

can all be exponentially small for a fixed \(R\), while the anisotropy obstruction remains live.

---

# 4. Can finite \(\xi\)-like models realize finite-order flatness?

Yes, structurally.

The relevant local jets are

\[
q'',\quad q''',\quad q^{(4)},\quad \ldots,\quad q^{(R+2)}.
\]

A finite \(\xi\)-like packet model with sufficiently many quartet parameters can tune finitely many Taylor coefficients. The map from packet parameters to these jets is analytic. Each packet contributes a rational jet vector depending on its offset and width. With enough packets, the jet vectors are generically full rank.

So for any fixed \(R\), one expects to solve

\[
q''=\varepsilon,
\]

\[
q'''=0,
\]

\[
q^{(4)}=\text{chosen value},
\]

\[
\ldots
\]

\[
q^{(R+2)}=\text{chosen value},
\]

while preserving:

\[
q_0>0,\quad g,h>0,\quad \text{quartet symmetry},\quad \text{same-scale core}.
\]

This is the same finite-dimensional interpolation mechanism that already produced the source-weighted countermodel.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite-order source-jet non-concentration is false in flexible finite models.}
}
\]

At minimum, it is not available from the current local structure.

---

# 5. Fixed-prime version

The fixed-prime model has

\[
\delta(t)=q''(t)
\]

as a trigonometric polynomial or AFE-compatible trigonometric sum.

The proposed theorem would require

\[
\max_{0\le \ell\le R}|\delta^{(\ell)}(t)|
\ge Q^{-A}
\]

whenever anisotropy is live.

But trigonometric polynomials can have high-order near-flat near-zeros. In a random-phase model, the vector

\[
(\delta,\delta',\ldots,\delta^{(R)})
\]

is an \((R+1)\)-dimensional observable. The event

\[
|\delta^{(\ell)}|<\varepsilon
\quad
0\le \ell\le R
\]

has probability roughly

\[
\varepsilon^{R+1}
\]

if the joint density is nondegenerate.

Over \(T=e^Q\) samples, the expected minimum scale is roughly

\[
\varepsilon_{\min}\sim T^{-1/(R+1)}=e^{-Q/(R+1)}.
\]

This is still smaller than every \(Q^{-A}\).

Thus even fixed-prime random heuristics suggest:

\[
\boxed{
\max_{\ell\le R}|\nabla^\ell H|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

is unlikely for any fixed finite \(R\).

---

# 6. Infinite derivative tower?

One might consider all derivatives:

\[
H,H',H'',\ldots.
\]

For an analytic function, if all derivatives vanish at a point, then \(H\) vanishes identically in a neighborhood. So an infinite tower would detect source collapse unless \(H\equiv0\).

But a proof cannot use infinitely many finite-core coordinates directly. It would need a quantitative analytic theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\max_{0\le \ell\le R(Q)}
|\nabla^\ell H|
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

with \(R(Q)\) growing appropriately.

This becomes a Remez/Cartan or analytic continuation problem for the source factor. It again requires:

1. complex neighborhood control;
2. growth bounds;
3. a non-tiny global coefficient/witness;
4. a bound on how large \(R(Q)\) must be.

This is a new architecture, not the current fixed finite-core route.

---

# 7. Projective/source-normalized escape revisited

Because finite derivative towers are vulnerable, the only clear way to remove source collapse is to divide out \(H\):

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}].
\]

This handles Regime B, where

\[
H\approx0,\qquad \max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\asymp1.
\]

But it does not handle Regime A, where

\[
\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}\approx0
\]

while \(H\asymp1\). For Regime A, the projective ratio is near \([0:0]\), i.e. undefined or unstable.

So source-normalization only solves the source-collapse half of the obstruction. It does not yield a full proof unless paired with a quantitative anisotropy separation theorem.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projectivization is not a complete escape.}
}
\]

It may still be useful as a diagnostic branch.

---

# 8. Consequence for current architecture

The current finite-core norm vector

\[
(A_7,A_9,\nabla A_7,\nabla A_9,\ldots,\nabla^R A_9)
\]

is vulnerable to source collapse for every fixed \(R\).

The needed theorem would be one of:

\[
\boxed{
\text{external arithmetic lower bound on finite source jets;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{analytic source nonflatness with }R(Q)\to\infty;
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective/source-normalized invariant plus anisotropy control;}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{new row not divisible by }H.
}
\]

No such row or theorem has been found.

---

# 9. Final classification

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\text{escape route} & \text{status} & \text{reason}\\
\hline
(A_7,A_9) & \text{fails} & \text{divisible by }H\\
\nabla(A_7,A_9) & \text{fails} & H'=O((q'')^2)\\
\nabla^2(A_7,A_9) & \text{conditional} & \text{detects only low-order source zero}\\
\nabla^R(A_7,A_9),\ R\text{ fixed} & \text{fails in finite models} & \text{source can be flat to order }R\\
\text{infinite derivative tower} & \text{not current architecture} & \text{requires analytic/global theorem}\\
\text{projective ratio} & \text{partial} & \text{handles source collapse, not anisotropy collapse}\\
\text{new non-}H\text{-divisible row} & \text{not found} & \text{open search}
\end{array}
\]

---

## Final conclusion

\[
\boxed{
\text{Transported derivative finite towers do not rescue the rank-two norm route.}
}
\]

The current architecture remains non-closing under the source-small Regime B unless one introduces:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a source-normalized/projective invariant}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuinely arithmetic source-jet nonflatness theorem.}
}
\]

The next best attack is no longer first transported derivatives. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{source-normalized/projective invariant branch}
}
\]

with the explicit warning that it must still address Regime A, where

\[
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})\approx(0,0).
\]

## Agent 6 — Projective Branch Synthesis / Decision

### Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The projective/source-normalized branch is useful diagnostically, but it is not yet a proof route.}
}
\]

Projectivization cancels the common source factor

\[
H:=yq'',
\]

so it directly addresses the source-collapse Regime B. However, it does **not** address Regime A, where the anisotropy vector itself collapses:

\[
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})\approx(0,0).
\]

Nor does it supply a global contradiction: there is still no canonical path, endpoint law, index, holonomy, or winding theorem forcing the projective curve to be incompatible with off-line packets. The attached instruction correctly frames this as the next test after the source-divisibility obstruction was identified. 

---

# 1. Decision table

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\text{issue} & \text{status} & \text{next action}\\
\hline
\text{extension through }H=0
& \text{formally yes if corrections are }H\text{-divisible}
& \text{requires correction audit}\\
\text{induced projective transport}
& \text{algebraically clean}
& \text{needs global projective equation}\\
\text{Regime A: }(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})\approx0
& \text{not solved}
& \text{requires higher anisotropy tower or quantitative bound}\\
\text{global projective contradiction}
& \text{missing}
& \text{major blocker}\\
\text{correction row stability}
& \text{conditional}
& \text{may require extra projective coordinates}\\
\text{finite-prime / finite-\(\xi\)-like adversaries}
& \text{still dangerous}
& \text{can likely tune projective direction}
\end{array}
\]

---

# 2. Projective ratio through source collapse

Assume the clean factorization

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=H G_1,
\qquad
A_9^{\mathfrak f}=H G_2,
\]

where

\[
G_1\sim \Gamma_G,
\qquad
G_2\sim \Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Then for \(H\ne0\),

\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}:A_9^{\mathfrak f}]
=
[G_1:G_2]
=
[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}].
\]

If

\[
H\to0,
\qquad
(G_1,G_2)\not\to(0,0),
\]

then the projective ratio has a clean limiting value:

\[
\boxed{
\lim_{H\to0}[A_7:A_9]=[G_1:G_2].
}
\]

So projectivization handles source-collapse Regime B **at the level of direction**.

But this is only true if correction rows are also \(H\)-divisible or negligible. If

\[
A_7=HG_1+R_7,
\qquad
A_9=HG_2+R_9,
\]

and \(R_7,R_9\) are not \(H\)-divisible, then near \(H=0\),

\[
[A_7:A_9]\approx [R_7:R_9],
\]

not

\[
[G_1:G_2].
\]

Thus the clean projective invariant requires either:

\[
\boxed{R_7,R_9\text{ are }H\text{-divisible}}
\]

or an enlarged projective coordinate system including correction rows.

---

# 3. Projective transport algebra

Let

\[
Z:=\frac{A_9}{A_7}.
\]

Using

\[
A_7=HG_1,
\qquad
A_9=HG_2,
\]

we compute

\[
\nabla Z
=
\frac{A_7\nabla A_9-A_9\nabla A_7}{A_7^2}.
\]

Now

\[
\nabla A_j=(\nabla H)G_j+H\nabla G_j.
\]

Therefore

\[
A_7\nabla A_9-A_9\nabla A_7
=
H^2(G_1\nabla G_2-G_2\nabla G_1).
\]

So

\[
\boxed{
\nabla Z
=
\frac{G_1\nabla G_2-G_2\nabla G_1}{G_1^2}.
}
\]

The common source factor cancels. Connection terms that act linearly on both rows also cancel in the usual projective connection, provided they act through the same rank-two connection.

Thus algebraically:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective transport cancels }H\text{ cleanly.}
}
\]

But this only gives a **projective ODE**. It does not give a contradiction unless we have global projective data.

---

# 4. Regime A remains fatal

Projectivization does not solve the anisotropy-collapse regime:

\[
H\asymp1,
\qquad
\Gamma_G\approx0,
\qquad
\Gamma_{G,2}\approx0.
\]

Then

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]
\]

is near

\[
[0:0],
\]

which is undefined. A blow-up can record the direction of approach, but it cannot create magnitude.

To address Regime A projectively, one would need a higher anisotropy projective tower:

\[
[\Gamma_{G,1}:\Gamma_{G,2}:\cdots:\Gamma_{G,R}],
\]

where

\[
\Gamma_{G,1}:=\Gamma_G.
\]

But for fixed \(R\), finite-prime or finite-\(\xi\)-like models can likely tune

\[
\Gamma_{G,1},\ldots,\Gamma_{G,R}
\]

all small. So this becomes the same problem again, shifted to a higher tower.

The likely requirement would be an \(R(Q)\)-growing or infinite anisotropy tower, with analytic control. That is a new architecture.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projectivization handles Regime B but not Regime A.}
}
\]

---

# 5. No global projective contradiction is known

A projective route would need a theorem involving the curve

\[
m\mapsto [\Gamma_G(m):\Gamma_{G,2}(m)]
\in \mathbb P^1.
\]

Possible global mechanisms include:

\[
\text{winding},
\quad
\text{monodromy},
\quad
\text{Maslov index},
\quad
\text{holonomy},
\quad
\text{boundary endpoint constraints},
\quad
\text{forced crossing}.
\]

But none is currently available.

The existing local framework does not provide:

\[
\boxed{
\text{canonical path}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{known endpoint projective values}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective index contradiction}.
}
\]

Finite \(\xi\)-like and fixed-prime models can likely realize flexible projective curves, because \((\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})\) are just local jet combinations. So any projective contradiction would require actual zeta arithmetic, just like the norm route.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective branch is currently diagnostic, not proof-closing.}
}
\]

---

# 6. Corrections may force enlarged coordinates

The clean ratio

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]
\]

requires correction rows not to dominate near \(H=0\).

If the rows are

\[
A_7=HG_1+R_7,
\qquad
A_9=HG_2+R_9,
\]

then projectivization is stable only if:

\[
R_7,R_9=o(H)
\]

or

\[
R_7,R_9
\]

are themselves \(H\)-divisible and fit into the \(G\)-coordinates.

If some correction row is source-robust, then it is not a nuisance: it may be the source-robust row we were searching for. But if it is background/normalization data rather than off-line packet-visible data, it may destroy the clean invariant without helping.

The honest projective object may therefore be enlarged:

\[
[A_7:A_9:R_{\rm corr}^{(7)}:R_{\rm corr}^{(9)}:\cdots].
\]

That is possible but again creates a new architecture.

---

# 7. Final classification

\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
\text{question} & \text{answer}\\
\hline
\text{Does projectivization cancel }H? & \text{yes, formally}\\
\text{Does it extend through }H=0? & \text{yes if corrections are controlled}\\
\text{Does it solve source-collapse Regime B?} & \text{directionally yes}\\
\text{Does it solve anisotropy-collapse Regime A?} & \text{no}\\
\text{Is there a global projective contradiction?} & \text{not known}\\
\text{Do finite models threaten it?} & \text{yes}\\
\text{Is it a proof route now?} & \text{no}
\end{array}
\]

---

# 8. Recommendation

\[
\boxed{
\text{Do not switch fully to the projective branch yet.}
}
\]

The projective branch is valuable as a diagnostic and may eventually be needed, but it does not presently close the proof. The immediate next useful theorem would have to be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Projective layered transport theorem.}
}
\]

It would need to prove all of the following:

1. clean source-factor cancellation, including correction rows;
2. extension across \(H=0\);
3. a projective transport equation for
   \[
   [\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}];
   \]
4. a global path/index/boundary law;
5. protection against Regime A, likely via a higher anisotropy tower.

That is a large new architecture.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Projectivization cancels the source factor but does not produce an RH contradiction.}
}
\]

The current architecture still lacks one of:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a source-robust norm row,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{an arithmetic source/anistropy lower bound,}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuine global projective index theorem.}
}
\]

So my recommendation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{treat the projective branch as diagnostic, while the main proof route remains blocked absent new arithmetic or global projective input.}
}
\]
